I truly wonder what people think life was like in the 18th century.  An 18th Century kitchen had wood/coal-fired stoves. White wheat flour has been milled by humans since 6000BC, but was usually reserved for the upper-crust of society, while the common man would have whole-grain flour. Love you, Sohla, and glad to see you back on YT.   I suspect my M&C recipe is closest to the original - it consists of macaroni (which WAS a thing back then, Thomas Jefferson served it in the White House), cheese, light cream and salt & pepper..... Amazingly delicious, and fast as lightning to make.   No bechamel, etc, because the French  "mother sauces" were not really a big thing until later, the 19th century with Antonin Careme. If you want to fancy my recipe up, add 2-3 oz of Greek yogurt to add a bit of tang, with a pinch or two of cayenne powder, a spare pinch of garlic and onion powder, and a pinch of thyme and you have gourmet M&C.

I don't really like Basics or any of the other stuff - I just like the old-style videos about true-to-form and perfect versions of film food.  I have become a lot less likely to click on a Babish video because they so often are not what I'm interested in anymore.  I realize that a lot of people are just stuck on the personality now, but I'm not really a 'cult of personality' kind of person.  I just want to say that I prefer for 'Babish' to stay true to its roots.  If it does, I'll be more likely to watch.  And I appreciate that the original host is trying to capitalize on his current fame and make as much money as possible before his popularity runs out, but this strategy is just making the clock run out faster.  What kind of world do you want to live in - one where neat movie food videos come out every week for YEARS, or one where we see the same guy making ads three times a week for maybe a year or so? Some of y'all are just... it was the 1700s, not the stone age. I mean... many rural people maybe wouldn't have access to super fine-ground flour grinder and stuff, but like, it did exist. A variety of pastas already existed, too, and were even being imported and all that stuff. Towsends has a video on 18th century pasta thats really cool. And hearths were a thing, and cast iron stoves were greatly increasing in popularity, so it's not like an open fire pit is the only option. Also some people are saying in the comments that "cheese would be hard to come by". Anyone remotely rural would've had at least one family ungulate for milking and cheese making, and commerce in urban setting would def make it available (more so than many things due to the fact that it's very nature mean it is preserved). This doesn't mean it would be cheap, but it would likely be at least accessible. Not to say Sohla was wrong in any of her choices (love her!), but IDK I feel i'm just picking up on a general misconception from some.

Fun fact! There is evidence that Thomas Jefferson, during his presidency (which admittedly started in 1801) served Mac' and Cheese to dinner guests. He had a macaroni crank imported from Napoli, and made (or more likely had made for him) a dish with macaroni, Parmesan cheese, bourbon, and onions (maybe) in a pie shell.  His macaroni recipe did indeed include eggs (but he was rich so he could afford stuff like that).

That was actually great Sp√§tzle, the dough was a bit on the thick side for shaving them. You want it just barely too thick to flow off the board, then use a knife to shave off the sp√§tzle with quick up and down motions. With a bit of practice you can probably shave that amount of sp√§tzle in one go without needing to skim the ones that are ready. The way I know K√§sesp√§tzle is without the bechamel, the pasta fried with the cheese in a pan so both the pasta and cheese get some crispy bits. But the goopy mess is A) more mac&cheese and B) probably more like how it would be made historically.

There WAS macaroni in the 18th century, one early American fanatic was that moron Thomas Jefferson. By the 1790s he had his own personal macaroni pasta machine imported from Europe that moved along with his estates, and his personal 18th c recipe is available on Monticello's website. The pasta was made with eggs, flour, water, and salt, then cut and hand rolled, very close to Sohla's technique! He liked serving it to guests at Monticello and while he was president.  I adore this and love seeing Sohla finally appreciated for her skill, but if you're going to have a segment of the wheel devoted to 18th C style cooking, I beg that you consult a food historian. So many old recipes are absolutely buck wild and fun to try figuring out from the archives!  [I interned at Monticello for multiple years. I hate Jefferson.]

The first modern recipe for macaroni and cheese was included in Elizabeth Raffald's 1770 book, The Experienced English Housekeeper. Raffald's recipe is for a B√©chamel sauce with cheddar cheese‚Äîa Mornay sauce in French cooking‚Äîwhich is mixed with macaroni, sprinkled with Parmesan, and baked until bubbly and golden. Another recipe from 1784 stated that the small tubes of macaroni must be boiled, then drained in a sifter before being moved to a frying pan. Heavy cream is then added to the macaroni along with a "knob of butter" rolled in flour, and it must be cooked for five minutes before being transferred to a dish and topped with toasted Parmesan and pepper.[8] The famous British Victorian cookbook Mrs Beeton's Book of Household Management included two instances of "Macaroni, as usually served with the Cheese Course". Up front, I enjoyed this. But I'm also disappointed. I wish, if she was going to make 18th century mac and cheese, that she might have done at least a little bit of research on 18th century cooking.  Cooking was generally done in a kitchen - in an oven or a purpose-built hearth. Fine flour was common. So were salt, pepper, and a variety of spices. Macaroni and cheese is mentioned in a cookbook from 1390 Denim and corduroy both existed (but cotton was expensive) They did not typically have communal ovens. If you didn't have a bread oven then you bought bread at the market.  I work at an 18th century living history museum and have spent many a happy (and tasty) hour hanging out in the kitchens where these techniques are employed every day.

Fun fact: In the 18th century, stylish young upper class men would travel to Italy,  often enjoying Macaroni pasta and bringing the idea back with them to England. This lead to the term "Macaroni" taking on a connotation similar to "Hipster" today, a sort of stuck up, upper class stylishness.  That's why, in the song "Yankee Doodle Dandy", Yankee Doodle put a feather in his hat and called it "macaroni"; the song was originally making fun of the Revolutionaries for acting like they're sophisticated Europeans with enlightenment values. The Americans, being Americans, turned it into a patriotic song out of spite.

A 15th century Italian chef named Martino de Rossi wrote a cookbook called "Libro de Arte Coquinaria" which included a recipe for Maccaroni Romaneschi; a pasta cooked in water, drained, and then butter, cheese, and spices added. Pretty much the earliest reference for what could easily be called macaroni and cheese and the recipe really hasn't changed all that much in 500 years.

Love Sohla and Babish and I am really excited to see more of this show. That said, if any of the production staff are combing the comments for feedback, I felt like this format was a little... incoherent. The concept is good, Sohla is charming, but I felt a little bit lost watching it. The research step was missing where Sohla explains what she is going to try to do. The cooking process looked so disorganized and uncomfortable that I had a trouble following along. For example, what was that pasta substitute? I'm not familiar with the name. I wanted to see it up close. What was its texture like? Also what happened to the hot dogs?! Were those legit 18th century hot dogs? Did hot dogs exist in the 18th century? I hunger for weird trivia.  I think this is where Bon Appetit's video production skill shone, with producers asking the chefs questions, so they could film the sound bites they needed to weave a coherent story. Popping up info onscreen when something important was omitted. IMO, that stuff was missing here.   Everyone comes to vids for something different, so this is just what I thought was missing. Maybe most people don't care about this stuff and it's just me. Who knows. Anyway, best of luck, and I look forward to the next one.

Traveling to Italy was very popular among wealthy and/or aristocratic university students‚Äîparticularly those from Great Britain‚Äîin the 18th Century. They would travel to Italy and study art and architecture with keen interest in classical antiquity: this is at least partially where neoclassical architecture has its roots. Upon returning home from their travels, these men would often have adopted dandyish fashion and manners of speech acquired abroad and desire to eat the same foods they enjoyed on the continent: macaroni among them. These people came to be known derisively as "macaronis."
